   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d40001553" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na superionic conductor (NASICON)-structured compounds demonstrate great application potential by their robust framework and compositional diversity. However, they are blamed for the mediocre energy density, and achieving both multielectron reaction and good cycling stability simultaneously is challenging. Herein, a novel heterogeneous Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)/Na<sub>2</sub>VTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NFPP/NVTP) material with stable multielectron reaction is constructed by spray drying technology. The mutual promotion effect of intergrowth structures effectively improves the purity and the crystallization of NFPP/NVTP during the fabrication process, which is beneficial to the high capacity and cycling stability. As a result, the optimized NFPP/NVTP demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 155.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and outstanding cycling stability with 82.9% capacity retention over 2500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which are much superior to those of NFPP and NVTP individually. Even in full cell configuration, the energy density remains high at ≈380&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the cathode mass. The high capacity of NFPP/NVTP is also attributed to the successive reduction/oxidation mechanism involving the introduction of Ti<sup>3+</sup> and interfacial charge redistribution effect between the heterogeneous phases, which greatly improve the electronic and ionic conductivity. Moreover, high reversible structural evolution during the multisodium storage process further guarantees excellent cycling stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202408918-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202408918-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are recognized as one of the ideal choices to meet the expanding demands for large-scale applications, because of their abundant resources and analogous electro-chemistry to lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0001_R_d40001408e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#adma202408918-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0003_R_d40001408e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among all components of SIBs, however, cathode materials are usually confronted with more challenges, such as sluggish reaction kinetics, and unstable crystal structure. Recent studies about sodium-ion cathodes mainly involve layered metal oxides, Prussian blue analogues, and polyanionic materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0004_R_d40001408e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - eScience">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through multiple comparisons, polyanionic materials featuring Na superionic conductor (NASICON) structure have a decided advantage given robust covalent frameworks and large compositional flexibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0005_R_d40001408e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Vanadium-based NASICONs-type materials have been widely documented. Especially, the typical representative Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> can exhibit a modest energy density of about 380&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> due to a high operating voltage (3.4&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and theoretical specific capacity (117.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0006_R_d40001408e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nanoscale">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a series of issues such as cost and toxicity associated with a high vanadium proportion will be greatly amplified in mass production and commercial application. For this reason, some novel vanadium-based NASICONs with different combinations of transition metal cations (VTi, VMn, VFe, etc.)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0007_R_d40001408e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ECS Electrochem. Lett.">7</a>-<a href="#adma202408918-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0009_R_d40001408e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Carbon">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been proposed and investigated. The high specific capacity (187 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) endowed by reversible three-electron reaction makes Na<sub>2</sub>VTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> a competitive candidate as sodium-ion cathode despite low (de)sodiation potential is still somewhat controversial.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0010_R_d40001408e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, many researchers pay attention to iron-based polyanionic materials due to their environmental friendliness and sustainable reserves. Among them, the mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) significantly stands out by virtue of relatively higher operating voltage due to the strong inductive effect of pyrophosphate (P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) groups, and faster ion diffusion kinetics promoted by open structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0011_R_d40001408e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) rarely undergoes complex phase transition reactions, finally leading to stable performance expression after extended cycles. Nevertheless, major obstacles in Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) system include low electronic conductivity and the generation of inactive impurities (maricite-type NaFePO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), which directly determine that its reversible capacity is still far from expectations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0012_R_d40001408e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Currently, some efforts have been made to improve the electronic conductivity of polyanionic materials. Although carbon coating appears to be a promising strategy, graphene or carbon nanotubes with high conductivity are not suitable for practical applications due to the complexity and high cost of the processing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0010_R_d40001408e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0013_R_d40001408e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Eng. J.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0014_R_d40001408e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Power Sources">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, for NFPP system, it is difficult for biomass carbon sources to form a well-graphitized carbon coating during the low-temperature calcination process, resulting in limited enhancement of conductivity. Furthermore, ion doping is also effective in improving electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0015_R_d40001408e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0016_R_d40001408e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Previous reports have demonstrated that Al<sup>3+</sup> or Cr<sup>3+</sup> doping can activate the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> (4.0&nbsp;V) redox reactions in vanadium-based cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0017_R_d40001408e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Res.">17</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0018_R_d40001408e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The evolved high- and medium-entropy cathodes, achieved through the synergistic incorporation of diverse metal ion substitutions, successfully stabilize multielectron redox chemistry.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0019_R_d40001408e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Sci.">19</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0020_R_d40001408e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, this modification strategy may come at the expense of capacity or cost advantages.</p>
         
         <p>Heterostructure has stimulated interest ever since the first patent concerned with p-n junction transistors proposed by Shorkley in 1951.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0021_R_d40001408e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Rev. Mod. Phys.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Different from traditional single component that suffers from unique limitations, heterogeneous composite materials can achieve the optimization and multifunctionality of performance. Additionally, due to the difference in Fermi energy levels, the intergrown heterostructures of the two phases will spontaneously generate internal electric fields, thereby promoting the redistribution and transfer of charge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0022_R_d40001408e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the concept of heterostructure has been extended to various application areas including sensor technology and energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0023_R_d40001408e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, the evolved heterogeneous oxides, sulfides, metal oxides/conductive polymers, etc., composite materials were reported as electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0024_R_d40001408e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - eScience">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhang et&nbsp;al. first developed a heterostructure iron-based sulfate cathode, in which the ionic kinetics of the main phase (Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) inside the particle bulk was improved by introducing a minor phase (Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>) with high ionic-conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0025_R_d40001408e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, Guo et&nbsp;al. synthesized a double carbon-layer decorated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) heterogeneous composite, achieving a reversible capacity of 113.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C with the median working voltage of 3.16&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0026_R_d40001408e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite the advances, the capacities of these heterogeneous polyanionic cathodes are still restricted by two electrons per unit formula. From the perspective of energy density, developing heterogeneous cathodes with reversible multielectron reactions is vital. Besides, it remains an urgent challenge to decipher a series of complex issues involving the inherent synergistic effect, and structural evolution mechanisms during the electrochemistry process.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we synthesized a heterogeneous Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)/Na<sub>2</sub>VTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NFPP/NVTP) cathode in one step via a spray-drying process. Benefiting from the mutual promotion effect of these two components, impurities in the NFPP phase are eliminated and the NVTP phase crystallizes at a low calcination temperature, which facilitates ion diffusion and structural stability. More importantly, the structural evolution of heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP cathode during the multisodium storage process is highly reversible, as confirmed by in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD). Both experimental and theoretical analyses revealed the redistribution of interfacial charge and a significant reduction in the bandgap, greatly improving the electronic conductivity upon the formation of the heterostructure and Ti<sup>3+</sup>. As expected, the optimized NFPP/NVTP affords a prominent energy density and outstanding cycling stability. Specifically, successive redox processes endow the NFPP/NVTP cathode with a high capacity of 155.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (three-electron reactions), thus achieving a remarkable energy density of up to 417&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, the HC//NFPP/NVTP full cells also demonstrate superior electrochemical performance, which confirms the application of this kind of heterogeneous material.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202408918-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202408918-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Several samples of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (NFPP), Na<sub>2</sub>VTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVTP), and heterogeneous composite Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)/Na<sub>2</sub>VTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NFPP/NVTP) were synthesized by a simple spray-drying route and the subsequent calcination process as described in the Experimental Section. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of NFPP/NVTP, which consists of crystal orthorhombic NFPP phase (space group: Pn21a) and the rhombohedral NVTP phase (space group: R-3c).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0010_R_d40001408e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0011_R_d40001408e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further Rietveld refinement reveals a quantified two-phase ratio of 1:1, indicating the formation of a composite phase without any impurities regardless of the complexity of the composition. The detailed information on cell parameters, atom locations, and occupations is recorded in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). As calculated by inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP), the atomic ratio (6.05:1.00:0.94:2.91:7.10) of Na, V, Ti, Fe, and P in NFPP/NVTP is consistent with the designed chemical composition (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). For comparison, the XRD patterns for homogeneous NFPP and NVTP materials prepared by similar processes and raw materials are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1b,c</a>. Different from NFPP/NVTP material with high crystallinity and purity, impurity phases (Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and NaFePO<sub>4</sub>) can be clearly detected in homogeneous NFPP. As reported in some previous studies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0027_R_d40001408e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Electrochim. Acta">27</a>-<a href="#adma202408918-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0029_R_d40001408e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> these inactive impurities always exist in the NFPP system during the synthetic procedure, which will obstacle ion transport and cause a capacity loss in electrochemical cycling. The key to reducing the amount of impurities may be lowering the Fe concentration at the beginning of the synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0012_R_d40001408e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0029_R_d40001408e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the XRD pattern of NVTP reveals its amorphous nature, despite the identical calcination temperature (600&nbsp;°C) applied to all three materials. ICP result confirms that NVTP has been synthesized according to the specified composition. Based on this, the crystallization conditions of NVTP were further explored, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). When the calcination temperature exceeds 650&nbsp;°C, NVTP begins to crystallize gradually. Otherwise, it remains in an amorphous state. However, NFPP materials usually crystallize at lower temperatures (500–600&nbsp;°C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0028_R_d40001408e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the NFPP phase crystallizes initially, serving as the crystalline nucleus to facilitate the crystallization of NVTP, which is the fundamental reason for obtaining well-crystallized NFPP/NVTP composite material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0030_R_d40001408e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Glass Sci. J. Non-Cryst. Solids">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, NVTP remains a solid solution in the early formation process of NFPP phase, which offers additional Na and P sources for the NFPP phase, thus reducing the relative concentration of Fe to inhibit the production of impurities. As we know, high purity and high crystallinity are prerequisites for electrode materials to achieve high electrochemical reactivity and long cycle life. In addition, the NVTP phase and NFPP phase growing adjacent to each other during the crystallization process will form abundant contact heterojunctions, as confirmed by HRTEM results discussed later.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202408918-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/25881aaf-b509-4758-bc54-c541ede7662d/adma202408918-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/25881aaf-b509-4758-bc54-c541ede7662d/adma202408918-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f50e2bc6-9563-4976-964d-7ca2ce6498a7/adma202408918-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/25881aaf-b509-4758-bc54-c541ede7662d/adma202408918-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408918-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rietveld refinement profile of NFPP/NVTP. The XRD patterns of b) NFPP and c) NVTP. The insets are the atomic ratios of Na, V, Ti, Fe, and P elements in NFPP/NVTP, NFPP, and NVTP, respectively. d) FT-IR spectra and e) electric conductivity of three materials. The comparison of high-resolution f) V 2p, g) Fe 2p, and h) Ti 2p spectra of NFPP/NVTP and MIX.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) is employed to further analyze the structure features of the as-synthesized materials by stretching vibration detection (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1d</a>). The characteristic peaks from 698 to 977 cm<sup>−1</sup> represent the symmetric and antisymmetric P–O–P vibrations of P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> groups. The PO<sub>4</sub> groups can be fingerprinted in the range of 400–690 and 1000–1310 cm<sup>−1</sup>, associated with O–P–O bending and P–O stretching vibrations, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0031_R_d40001408e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Power Sources">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Multiple absorption peaks in NFPP/NVTP and NFPP verify the abundance of P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and PO<sub>4</sub> groups in both structures. The NVTP material exhibits relatively flat absorption peaks due to its flexible amorphous structure. In addition, the signals located at 420–580 and 960–1090 cm<sup>−1</sup> in Raman spectra further confirm the existence of PO<sub>4</sub> and P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> groups (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0032_R_d40001408e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Storage Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relative intensities of D-band and G-band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values) are all less than 1, indicating a high degree of graphitization in the carbon coatings of all samples. Such a graphitization nature will result in enhanced electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0033_R_d40001408e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The resistivity test further shows the conductivity results of the samples under different pressures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1e</a>). The measured electric conductivity of NFPP/NVTP is 0.124 S m<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;MPa, which is 1–2 orders of magnitude higher than that of the other two samples. The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) shows that the three samples have similar carbon content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the significant enhancement in conductivity should be attributed to the beneficial effects of the heterogeneous structure.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to further study the valance states of three metal elements. The existence of Na, V, Ti, Fe, P, O, and C elements is identified in the full spectrum of NFPP/NVTP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution V 2p spectrum can be deconvoluted into two characteristic peaks located at 523.57&nbsp;eV (V 2p1/2) and 516.99&nbsp;eV (V 2p3/2) respectively, along with a satellite peak, indicating a trivalent oxidation state of V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1f</a>). Fe in NFPP/NVTP presents a mixed valance state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1g</a>). Specifically, the peaks at 724.42&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p1/2) and 710.87&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p3/2) are characteristic peaks of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and weak peaks at 726.41&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p1/2) and 713.28&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p3/2) are characteristic peaks of Fe<sup>3+</sup>. It is noteworthy that Fe elements are theoretically divalent (Fe<sup>2+</sup>) in NFPP. The existence of Fe<sup>3+</sup> may be due to the exposure of surface to air, which is also observed in previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0034_R_d40001408e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0035_R_d40001408e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - New J. Chem.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the Ti 2p XPS spectrum is fitted into four peaks, among which the stronger peaks at 465.96&nbsp;eV (Ti 2p1/2) and 460.24&nbsp;eV (Ti 2p3/2) are ascribed to Ti<sup>4+</sup> species, while the weaker peaks at 464.33&nbsp;eV (Ti 2p1/2) and 458.68&nbsp;eV (Ti 2p3/2) correspond to Ti<sup>3+</sup> species (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1h</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0016_R_d40001408e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The emergence of Ti<sup>3+</sup> can be attributed to the interfacial charge interference of different phases in intergrown structures, which will be verified later. As we know, Ti<sup>3+</sup> with unpaired electrons can introduce additional electrons as carriers, thereby increasing the electronic conductivity of the NFPP/NVTP material. For instance, relevant work has confirmed that inducing Ti<sup>3+</sup> species or oxygen vacancies obviously reduced the bandgap of TiO<sub>2</sub> and facilitated the electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0036_R_d40001408e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To better analyze the influence of heterogeneous structure on electric charge, the physical mixing of NVTP and NFPP materials with a mass ratio of 1:1 was used as a reference (denoted as MIX). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0001">1h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), MIX presents the same valence states as the metal elements in amorphous NVTP and homogeneous NFPP, especially Ti, which is tetravalent. Notably, the apparent differences in XPS spectra confirm the close relationship and interaction between NVTP phase and NFPP phase in heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP material. In contrast, physical mixture samples typically exhibit the spectral characteristics of the original components due to the absence of these interface effects.</p>
         
         <p>Morphological features of NFPP/NVTP, NFPP, and NVTP are observed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM). Several materials exhibit spherical particle morphology with micrometer-scale size distribution (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). As further indicated by the enlarged SEM image, individual NVTP particles are dispersive with rough surfaces, whereas NFPP particles are gathered with relatively smooth surfaces (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). Remarkably, the secondary NFPP/NVTP particles are assembled by a large number of nanoparticles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0002">2c</a>). The adjacent nanoparticles are loosely interconnected with sufficient mesoporous. Correspondingly, NFPP/NVTP exhibits the smallest average pore diameter of 8.372&nbsp;nm, as determined by the Barret-Joyner-Halenda (BJH) method (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Due to differences in crystallinity and morphology, NFPP/NVTP exhibits a drastically different pore-size distribution pattern compared to the other two materials. Besides, such a hierarchical structure will endow NFPP/NVTP with higher electrolyte infiltration. Other SEM images of NFPP/NVTP, NFPP, and NVTP are given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). The TEM images further confirm that the in situ generated conductive carbon network of 3–4&nbsp;nm is continuously anchored on the surface of these materials (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). TEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information) also demonstrate convincingly that NVTP is amorphous. Generally, such amorphous materials feature intricate microstructures, such as pores, roughness, and other microscopic characteristics, leading to a significant increase in specific surface area (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0037_R_d40001408e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - CCS Chem.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> TEM-based energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mappings display that all the elements are distributed in NFPP/NVTP, in accordance with the XPS results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0002">2d</a>). Additionally, element (V, Ti, and Fe) line scanning in TEM images was carried out to investigate the phase distribution in NFPP/NVTP particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0002">2e</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0002">2f</a>, the abundance of Fe always corresponds to the scarcity of V and Ti, and vice versa. This is direct evidence of the cross-distribution of NFPP and NVTP in the heterostructure, further indicating that numerous heterointerfaces exist to maximize the synergistic effect of the whole heterostructure. The atomic-scale observation of the fine structure of NFPP/NVTP material was detected by the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images. There are two distinct crystal orientations in one region with tight contact and a distinct interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0002">2g</a>). The inter-planar spacing of 3.77 Å can be ascribed to the (113) crystal plane of NVTP and the lattice stripes of 5.40 Å can match well with the (002) crystal plane of NFPP. Considering HRTEM does have some limitations related to its narrow field of view, additional HRTEM images of NFPP/NVTP are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a> (Supporting Information). By contrast, the heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP material possesses abundant heterojunctions that are significantly different from homogeneous materials (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>. Supporting Information). However, SEM-EDS mappings of MIX demonstrate that NFPP and NVTP are dispersed separately (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0002">2h</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202408918-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1d94642c-783e-4ff7-8137-1524096e8cfc/adma202408918-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1d94642c-783e-4ff7-8137-1524096e8cfc/adma202408918-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/889b1fe9-5e80-4c3b-9f57-d16976fd148a/adma202408918-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1d94642c-783e-4ff7-8137-1524096e8cfc/adma202408918-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408918-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The SEM images of a) NVTP, b) NFPP, and c) NFPP/NVTP. d) EDS mapping images of NFPP/NVTP. e) The TEM image of the element line scanning area and f) the corresponding scanning results of NFPP/NVTP. g) The HRTEM image of heterostructure in NFPP/NVTP. h) EDS mapping and SEM image of MIX.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves between 1.5 and 4.3&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of Na half cells using three cathodes in the first three cycles are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3a</a>. Highly overlapped CV profiles indicate decent electrochemical reversibility in sodiation/desodiation processes. As seen, NFPP presents two pairs of well-defined redox peaks (3.01/2.84&nbsp;V and 3.28/3.17&nbsp;V) and multiple weak peaks between 3.0 and 3.2&nbsp;V, corresponding to Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation/intercalation steps at different sites based on the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0038_R_d40001408e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0039_R_d40001408e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Storage Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curve of NFPP at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> delivers that its initial discharge capacity is only 71.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, ≈56% of the theoretical capacity (129 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information). The large gap in capacity arises from low intrinsic conductivity and electrochemically inactive impurities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0012_R_d40001408e1080" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the CV curve of the amorphous NVTP is totally different from the crystalline one. For instance, our previous studies have reported that the crystalline NVTP cathode displays three obvious voltage plateaus located at 1.6, 2.1, and 3.4&nbsp;V, which are from the V<sup>2+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup>, and V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox couples, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0040_R_d40001408e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Nano">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Of note, the amorphous NVTP shows sloping GCD profiles, consistent with broad redox peaks in the CV curves. Although its sodium storage behavior is more complex, NVTP achieves a 2.15-electron reaction, releasing a reversible capacity of 133.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0041_R_d40001408e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chin. J. Chem.">41</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0042_R_d40001408e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP cathode displays a discharge capacity of 155.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a three-electron redox reaction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3b</a>). CV curves of NFPP/NVTP show multiple pairs of redox peaks, in accordance with the GCD results. Interestingly, the new high-voltage plateaus corresponding to the redox couple of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> (4.10/4.08&nbsp;V) are activated. Other redox peaks associated with V<sup>2+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> (1.65/1.55&nbsp;V), Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> (2.22/2.09&nbsp;V), and V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> (3.42/3.32&nbsp;V) show a slightly right-shifted trend compared with the reported ones.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0010_R_d40001408e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0043_R_d40001408e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Storage Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the theory of the inductive effect, the increase in the average voltage of the NVTP phase is attributed to the stronger electronegativity of (P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sup>4−</sup> in the NFPP phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0044_R_d40001408e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Overall, the combination of high capacity and high operating potential highlights the superiority of the heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0045_R_d40001408e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Mater. Chem. A">45</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0046_R_d40001408e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Green Energy Environ.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202408918-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/728af4c3-fe08-4cc0-abd3-ee5b52587317/adma202408918-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/728af4c3-fe08-4cc0-abd3-ee5b52587317/adma202408918-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2901c12-4274-451c-97bb-ad669807fb8e/adma202408918-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/728af4c3-fe08-4cc0-abd3-ee5b52587317/adma202408918-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408918-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The CV curves of NFPP/NVTP (upper), NFPP (middle), and NVTP (lower). b) The GCD curves of NFPP/NVTP at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Cycling performance of as-prepared cathodes at c) 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and d) 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Rate performance, the corresponding f) median voltage and g) Ragone plot of as-prepared cathodes from 20 to 5000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3c</a> shows the cycling performance of these four cathodes at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. NFPP/NVTP attains an initial discharge capacity of 139.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is superior to those of NVTP (125.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and NFPP (65.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). After 150 cycles, it achieves a capacity retention of 90.9% with a Coulombic efficiency close to 100%. In contrast, NVTP exhibits a distinct capacity attenuation caused by the instability of amorphous phase. NFPP achieves a higher retention of 97.6%, but the low specific capacity severely weakens its competitiveness. As expected, the MIX cathode only reaches the average specific capacity level of NVTP and NFPP, which continues to decline as the cycle increases. At 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, NFPP/NVTP demonstrates outstanding cycling durability with 89.4% capacity retention after 1000 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Further increasing the current density to 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, NFPP/NVTP also outperforms other cathodes (NFPP, NVTP, and MIX) with a high initial discharge capacity of 121.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3d</a>). After 2500 cycles, a discharge capacity of 100.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is retained, corresponding to a desirable capacity retention of 82.9%. In fact, the deviation in electrochemical reaction potentials of the two phases forming the heterostructure efficaciously alleviates the lattice strain accumulation, ultimately enhancing cyclic stability. Therefore, the heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP cathode successfully integrates the respective advantages of NFPP and NVTP phases due to their synergistic effect, demonstrating excellent electrochemical performance. Unfortunately, MIX inherits both the low capacity of NFPP and the instability of NVTP, which means that no good interaction occurs between NVTP and NFPP with simple mixing.</p>
         
         <p>The superiority of heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP cathode is further clarified by the rate performance test at various current densities ranging from 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> to 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3e</a>, NFPP/NVTP affords a much higher capacity at all rates than the other three cathodes. In particular, it expresses a reversible capacity of 97.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high rate of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which far exceeds that of NFPP (46.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), NVTP (19.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and MIX (1.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). When the current is increased above 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, MIX shows the lowest performance, which may be attributed to the greater polarization effect in simple physical mixing sample. After 45 cycles, its capacity recovers to the initial level (150.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), implying good reversibility for Na<sup>+</sup> storage process. The corresponding GCD curves also exhibit a smaller overpotential (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>), Supporting Information. As a significant contrast, the GCD curves of MIX exhibit both a slope region and a plateau region, embodying the straightforward mixing of two materials. Additionally, the median voltage variations, which serve as an important indicator of energy density, are recorded in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3f</a>. MIX experiences significant voltage attenuation, while NVTP operates in a relatively low voltage range (&lt;2.6&nbsp;V). Although the median potential of NFPP is slightly higher, NFPP/NVTP still demonstrates a much higher energy density (417&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) based on the mass of the cathode active materials due to its high specific capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3g</a>). In order to reinforce these findings, the highly crystalline NVTP and the MIX sample (a physical mixture of highly crystalline NVTP and NFPP, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) were prepared and compared to further confirm the synergistic effect of the heterogeneous material. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information, high crystallinity does endow NVTP and MIX with a more stable cycling performance. However, compared to NFPP/NVTP, they exhibit greater polarization, resulting in relatively low specific capacity and energy density.</p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage mechanism of NFPP/NVTP cathode during the charge/discharge process remains unclear. Identifying the phase evolution is crucial to understanding this type of reinforced behavior in the heterogeneous composite. We collected in situ XRD patterns to observe the overall information of NFPP/NVTP crystal structure within the voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information, multiple diffraction peaks undergo a series of periodical changes during the first two GCD cycles, demonstrating that NFPP/NVTP exhibits highly reversible sodium storage characteristics. Specifically, the structural evolution is explored by monitoring (211), (116), and (300) peaks of the NVTP phase and (022), (222), (420), and (004) peaks of the NFPP phase (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0004">4a</a>). In fact, the (116) peak of the NVTP phase and the (022) peak of the NFPP phase are both near 32°.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0047_R_d40001408e1274" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">47</a>-<a href="#adma202408918-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0049_R_d40001408e1274" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Alloys Compd.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The superposition of peaks results in higher intensity in the in situ XRD contour plot. During the initial charging stage, all diffraction peaks shift to a higher angle, indicating the lattice contraction due to Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. Subsequently, the (222) and (004) diffraction peaks of NFPP phase firstly shift to a lower angle before 3.25&nbsp;V and then abruptly direct toward a higher angle (i highlight). This state is attributed to the distortion of [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] units during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from Na1 and Na4 sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0038_R_d40001408e1288" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The entire process involves an imperfect one-phase reaction from Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) to Na<sub>1</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), accompanied by the processive oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0028_R_d40001408e1324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the (116) and (300) peaks of NVTP phase shift rightward upon charging, indicative of a topotactic one-phase reaction. However, the (211) peak progressively fades away, suggesting the formation of a new phase when entering the high voltage region, characteristic of a typical biphasic reaction. During the discharging stage, all the diffraction peaks of NFPP phase and NVTP phase revert to the pristine positions in an almost symmetrical manner. Subsequent cycles also prove that one-phase and biphasic reactions occur during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Obviously, the heterogeneous NFPP/NVTP material inherits the representative transitions of each phase composition.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202408918-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/242f80bf-b29f-4581-9a75-d28336c6d7c2/adma202408918-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/242f80bf-b29f-4581-9a75-d28336c6d7c2/adma202408918-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a4ddff10-ac7b-48d1-abc3-fd559137c451/adma202408918-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/242f80bf-b29f-4581-9a75-d28336c6d7c2/adma202408918-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408918-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) 2D contour maps of in situ XRD patterns of NFPP/NVTP for the first two cycles at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) The XRD pattern and c) HRTEM image of NFPP/NVTP electrode after long-term cycling.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The structural information of the electrodes after long-term cycling was further analyzed using SEM and XRD. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), the cycled NVTP cathode remains in amorphous status. However, the electrode surface of NVTP exhibits visible cracks and holes, suggesting that volume expansion causes serious stress accumulation during the cycling process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). This damage to the surface structure promotes the intrusion of the electrolyte, which leads to the corrosion of NVTP and the deterioration of the performance. The NFPP and NFPP/NVTP electrodes are relatively flat and intact. The weakening in the diffraction intensities of the cycled NFPP electrode may be attributed to minor internal structural looseness or deformation. No significant differences are observed in the XRD pattern of NFPP/NVTP before and after cycling, indicating that superior crystallinity is still well maintained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0004">4b</a>). The slight intensity difference in peaks like (011) and (202) might be due to varying charge states in the electrodes of pre- and postcycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0013_R_d40001408e1366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Eng. J.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202408918-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0050_R_d40001408e1366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nat. Commun.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the orientation of the particles may change during the cycling process, resulting in an increased exposure area of certain crystal planes, which in turn enhances the intensity of their diffraction peaks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0051_R_d40001408e1372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, the HRTEM image displays that the heterostructure between the NFPP phase and the NVTP phase with close contact is visible even after the ultralong cycling process, in which the lattice fringes of 3.75 and 3.17 Å match well the (212) plane of NFPP and the (024) plane of NVTP, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0004">4c</a>). The robust heterostructure of NFPP/NVTP is a key factor behind its excellent electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>The electrode process kinetics were investigated by CV tests and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). Specifically, CV tests were performed from 0.2 to 1.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> to determine the Na<sup>+</sup> apparent diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) of the individual redox pairs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). The related calculations are based on the Randles–Sevcik equation (Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) and involve the linear relationship between peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) against the square root of the scan rates (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202408918-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202408918-bib-0052_R_d40001408e1403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the CV curves, the calculation results show that the A0/C0 redox peaks of NFPP/NVTP have minimum <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> value, indicating the sodium storage process is mainly limited by the reaction step of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox. Even so, the activation of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> does work in NFPP/NVTP. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0005">5a</a>, the other <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> values of NFPP/NVTP are substantially greater than those of the NFPP and NVTP, implying more rapid diffusion kinetics occurred in the redox reactions (V<sup>2+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox couples). The specific calculation data are recorded in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5–S6</a> (Supporting Information). On account of the construction of heterogeneous structure, the full usability of transition metal elements promotes the multielectron transfer of NFPP/NVTP in the wide voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. At the same time, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was also employed after several activation cycles to analyze the variation trend of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> along with the time (calculation method is described in Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The GITT curves of three materials clearly convey information about their respective diffusion behaviors, capacity delivery, etc (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0005">5b</a>). Taking NFPP/NVTP as an example, we observe sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion at the end of the charge/discharge process, closely related to the redox of V<sup>4+</sup>/<sup>5+</sup>, which agrees well with the CV results. Compared with NFPP and NVTP, the diffusion behavior of NFPP/NVTP is more stable without obvious barriers. The calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> stays in the magnitude range of 10<sup>−8</sup> to 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> over the whole charge/discharge course. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> of NFPP drops sharply to 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at 3.0&nbsp;V, whose phase transition makes the diffusion process more difficult. Especially for NVTP cathode, there are greater fluctuations in <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> values within a relatively wide range of 10<sup>−8</sup> to 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. The inferior diffusion renders the fast capacity fading of NVTP as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0003">3c</a>. Overall, both CV and GITT tests manifest that the reaction kinetics of NFPP/NVTP electrodes are enhanced.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202408918-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7ade439c-feaf-4858-a6c2-378d14270ded/adma202408918-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7ade439c-feaf-4858-a6c2-378d14270ded/adma202408918-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bc0a4442-e1fb-4800-bac4-b4a76af9680a/adma202408918-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7ade439c-feaf-4858-a6c2-378d14270ded/adma202408918-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408918-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients calculated by a) CV curves and b) charge process in GITT curves. c) Charge density difference of NFPP (010)/NVTP (100) heterostructure from the side and top view, respectively. d) Density of states calculation results for NFPP/NVTP, NFPP, and NVTP. e) Schematic of synergistic effects in NFPP/NVTP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>First-principles calculations were then performed to investigate the interfacial charge behavior of the heterostructure in improving the electronic conductivity of NFPP/NVTP. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0005">5c</a>, the charge density difference was calculated by constructing the crystal structure of the NFPP (010)/NVTP (100) heterojunction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). Obviously, the charge distribution in the heterogeneous structure is unbalanced, accompanied by a charge transfer occurring from the NFPP side to NVTP side. Bader charge calculations further demonstrate that Fe and O lose 0.04 and 0.19 electrons, respectively, while V and Ti gain 0.01 and 0.23 electrons, respectively. There is a strong electrostatic field across the interface that induces electron transfer from NFPP to NVTP. As a result, the interfacial charge redistribution causes Ti elements to accumulate electrons from O, while V and Fe do not change significantly, which is consistent with high-resolution XPS spectra of NFPP/NVTP as discussed above. The redistribution of interfacial charge is the fundamental reason for the accelerated charge transfer. The charge transfer dynamics of the materials were studied by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and the fitting results can be seen in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information). NFPP/NVTP has the smallest charge transfer resistances, indicating the best charge transfer dynamics. In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0005">5d</a> depicts that the bandgap values of NFPP and NVTP are 3.16 and 1.42&nbsp;eV, respectively. Interestingly, within the NFPP/NVTP heterostructure, the density of states intersects with the Fermi level, indicating a significant restructuring of the electronic architecture at the interface. This intersection also implies a substantial enhancement in electronic conductivity upon the formation of the heterostructure.</p>
         
         <p>The above characterization and analysis results reveal that the introduction of two phases to construct heterogeneous composite materials is an effective modification strategy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0005">5e</a>). Especially through the interaction of two phases, the advantages of each are integrated to obtain excellent electrochemical performance. The NFPP/NVTP cathode material with high crystallinity was successfully prepared by using the precrystallized NFPP phase as the crystal nuclei. Also, postcrystallized NVTP effectively eliminated the generation of electrochemical inactive impurities (NaFePO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) with close framework, which are key factors in inhibiting ion diffusion. More importantly, the generation of Ti<sup>3+</sup> and activation of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox in the NVTP phase can effectively improve the electronic conductivity and energy density of NFPP/NVTP. Therefore, the optimized NFPP/NVTP realizes three-electron redox reactions with high reversibility. Meanwhile, the charge redistribution at the heterointerfaces can facilitate charge transfer and Na ion diffusion. The abundant electron distribution near the Fermi level indicates that NFPP/NVTP has a higher redox activity in the electrochemical reaction processes. The structural evolution involving one-phase and biphasic reactions for robust heterostructure is highly reversible and finally leads to the high-performance and long-life NFPP/NVTP.</p>
         
         <p>To fully grasp the superiority of heterostructure NFPP/NVTP cathode material, full cells were fabricated with NFPP/NVTP cathode and commercial hard carbon (HC) anode (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0006">6a</a>, denoted as HC//NFPP/NVTP), and were tested in a voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information), the HC anode exhibits a low initial Coulombic efficiency (84.5%), which will cause irreversible capacity loss of full cell. To enhance the performance, the HC anodes require presodiation treatment before assembling the full cells. Besides, the HC anode delivers a specific capacity of 233.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, which is ≈1.5 times higher than that of the cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0006">6b</a>). Due to the low operating potentials of HC anode, the GCD curves of HC//NFPP/NVTP display similar trends as those of the NFPP/NVTP cathode in half-cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0006">6c</a>). Based on the mass of cathode, the HC//NFPP/NVTP cell can afford a high specific capacity of 130.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a median voltage of ≈2.87&nbsp;V, enabling an impressive energy density of ≈389.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0006">6d</a> shows the cycling performance of the HC//NFPP/NVTP full cells at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. After 50 cycles, its discharge capacity is 120.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention rate of 92.2%. At a higher current density of 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the full cell displays more stable electrochemical performance, maintaining 92.7% of its initial specific capacity (125.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0006">6e</a>). In addition, the illuminated LED panel serves as a demonstration of the HC//NFPP/NVTP full cell in practical applications. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0006">6f</a> presents the excellent rate performance of HC//NFPP/NVTP full cell, with specific capacities of 137.7 and 95.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Analysis of the corresponding GCD curves reveals that capacity loss under high current density is primarily attributed to the disappearance of low-voltage platforms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202408918-fig-0006">6g</a>). Consequently, the HC//NFPP/NVTP full cell maintains a relatively high energy density range of ≈300–390&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of cathode) when the current density is less than 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The outstanding electrochemical performance clearly indicates the feasibility of NFPP/NVTP as an attractive SIB cathode for practical applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202408918-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b3ccbf3-693d-4111-afed-addd3c8faab7/adma202408918-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b3ccbf3-693d-4111-afed-addd3c8faab7/adma202408918-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/119a43d8-a1a4-4c45-bbbc-731c699201e7/adma202408918-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b3ccbf3-693d-4111-afed-addd3c8faab7/adma202408918-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202408918-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202408918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The schematical illustration of HC//NFPP/NVTP full cell. b) The GCD curves of NFPP/NVTP cathode (pink) and HC anode (yellow) in half cells. c) The GCD curves of HC//NFPP/NVTP full cell at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The cycling performance of HC//NFPP/NVTP full cell at d) 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and e) 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. f) The rate performance and g) the corresponding GCD curves. h) The median voltages and the energy densities of HC//NFPP/NVTP full cell at different current densities.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202408918-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202408918-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a novel NFPP/NVTP heterostructure cathode material was prepared by a facile spray-drying process. The mutual promotion of intergrowth structures rationalized the high crystallinity and high purity of NFPP/NVTP. Moreover, it was disclosed that the emergence of Ti<sup>3+</sup> and charge redistribution at the phase boundaries of NFPP/NVTP could contribute to decreasing forbidden bandgap and enhancing electronic conductivity. As a result, multiple redox couples (Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, V<sup>2+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>) activated in the optimized NFPP/NVTP cathode, which could realize a reversible three-electron redox reaction per formula. The NFPP/NVTP cathode exhibited high specific capacity and long cycling life, affording a discharge capacity of 155.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 82.9% capacity retention after 2500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The application potential of the NFPP/NVTP cathode was finally manifested by full cell tests, achieving a remarkable energy density of 389.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. The outstanding cycling stability should be attributed to the unique sodium storage mechanisms (one-phase and biphasic reactions) as confirmed by systematic electrochemical measurements, in situ XRD analyses, and DFT computations. The strategy of constructing heterogeneous structure provides a good reference for developing high-performance NASICON-type cathodes.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
